XHTML 1.0 Transitional//EN\">\n\n<div class=\"menu\" id=\"bloc\"><ul>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#top_full\">Top</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#abs\">Abstract</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S1\">1. Introduction</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S2\">2. Time Delay Challenge 1</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S3\" title=\"Stage I: discovering time delays\">3. Stage I: ...</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S6\" title=\"Stage II: measuring time delays\">4. Stage II: ...</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S9\">5. Results on the TDC1 data</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S13\">6. Summary and conclusions</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#ack\">Acknowledgments</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#ref\">References</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#app\">Appendix A:</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#tabs\">List of tables</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#figs\">List of figures</a></li>\n</ul></div>\n<div id=\"contenu\">\n<a name=\"top_full\"></a><div id=\"head\">A&amp;A 585, A88 (2016)<h2 class=\"title\">COSMOGRAIL: the COSmological MOnitoring   of GRAvItational Lenses</h2>\n<h3 class=\"subtitle\">XV. Assessing the achievability and precision of time-delay measurements</h3>\n<div class=\"article-authors\"><p class=\"bold\"><span id=\"aa26704-15-author-1\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201526704&amp;n=1\" class=\"author\">V. Bonvin</span><sup>1</sup>, <span id=\"aa26704-15-author-2\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201526704&amp;n=2\" class=\"author\">M. Tewes</span><sup>2</sup>, <span id=\"aa26704-15-author-3\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201526704&amp;n=3\" class=\"author\">F. Courbin</span><sup>1</sup>, <span id=\"aa26704-15-author-4\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201526704&amp;n=4\" class=\"author\">T. Kuntzer</span><sup>1</sup>, <span id=\"aa26704-15-author-5\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201526704&amp;n=5\" class=\"author\">D. Sluse</span><sup>3</sup> and <span id=\"aa26704-15-author-6\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201526704&amp;n=6\" class=\"author\">G. Meylan</span><sup>1</sup></p></div>\n<p class=\"aff\"><sup>1</sup> Laboratoire d&#8217;astrophysique, &#201;cole Polytechnique F&#233;d&#233;rale de Lausanne (EPFL), Observatoire de Sauverny,  1290   Versoix,  Switzerland \n<br>\n            e-mail: vivien.bonvin@epfl.ch\n            \n          <br><sup>2</sup>  Argelander-Institut f&#252;r Astronomie,  Auf dem H&#252;gel 71,  53121   Bonn,  Germany \n<br><sup>3</sup> Institut d&#8217;Astrophysique et de G&#233;ophysique, Universit&#233; de Li&#232;ge, All&#233;e du 6 Ao&#251;t 17, B5c,  4000   Li&#232;ge,  Belgium \n<br></p>\n<p class=\"history\"><span class=\"bold\">Received: </span>9 June 2015<br><span class=\"bold\">Accepted: </span>28 September 2015</p>\n<p class=\"bold\"><a name=\"abs\"></a>Abstract</p>\n        <p>COSMOGRAIL is a long-term photometric monitoring of gravitationally lensed quasars aimed at implementing Refsdal&#8217;s time-delay method to measure cosmological parameters, in particular <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>H</i><sub>0</sub></span>. Given the long and well sampled light curves of strongly lensed quasars, time-delay measurements require numerical techniques whose quality must be assessed. To this end, and also in view of future monitoring programs or surveys such as the LSST, a blind signal processing competition named Time Delay Challenge 1 (TDC1) was held in 2014. The aim of the present paper, which is based on the simulated light curves from the TDC1, is double. First, we test the performance of the time-delay measurement techniques currently used in COSMOGRAIL. Second, we analyse the quantity and quality of the harvest of time delays obtained from the TDC1 simulations. To achieve these goals, we first discover time delays through a careful inspection of the light curves via a dedicated visual interface. Our measurement algorithms can then be applied to the data in an automated way. We show that our techniques have no significant biases, and yield adequate uncertainty estimates resulting in reduced <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> values between 0.5 and 1.0. We provide estimates for the number and precision of time-delay measurements that can be expected from future time-delay monitoring campaigns as a function of the photometric signal-to-noise ratio and of the true time delay. We make our blind measurements on the TDC1 data publicly available.</p>\n      <div class=\"kword\"><p><span class=\"bold\">Key words: </span>methods: data analysis / gravitational lensing: strong / cosmological parameters</p></div>\n</div>\n<p><i>&#169; ESO, 2015</i></p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S1\"></a>1. Introduction</h2>\n<p>The methods used to constrain current cosmological models all benefit from independent measurements of the local value of the <i>Hubble </i>parameter, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>H</i><sub>0</sub></span> (see e.g. Fig. 48 of <a name=\"InR27\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R27\">Weinberg et al. 2013</a>). One way of achieving a measurement of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>H</i><sub>0</sub></span> is based on time delays in strong gravitational lens systems. The method, first suggested by <a name=\"InR18\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R18\">Refsdal (1964)</a>, proposes measuring the differences in the travel time of photons coming from multiple images of a distant source, such as a quasar. This time delay, <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>t</i></span>, is connected to the overall matter distribution responsible for the lensing effect, and to the time-delay distance <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>D</i><sub>&#916;<i>t</i></sub></span> to the lens, i.e. <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>H</i><sub>0</sub></span>, with some sensitivity to curvature and dark energy as well (e.g., <a name=\"InR22\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R22\">Suyu et al. 2014</a>). </p>\n<p>Exploiting this relationship to constrain <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>H</i><sub>0</sub></span> and cosmology in general requires both an accurate mass model for the lens and accurate time delay measurements (see e.g., <a name=\"InR21\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R21\">Suyu et al. 2012</a>; <a name=\"InR11\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R11\">Linder 2011</a>; <a name=\"InR15\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R15\">Moustakas et al. 2009</a>). Modelling the lens mass in an unbiased way is difficult and prone to degeneracies known as the mass-sheet degeneracy (e.g., <a name=\"InR19\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R19\">Schneider &amp; Sluse 2013</a>) and, more generally, the source plane transformation described in <a name=\"InR20\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R20\">Schneider &amp; Sluse (2014)</a>. The effect of lens model degeneracies can be mitigated by combining astrometric information from high resolution imaging, measurements of the lens dynamics, priors on the mass density profile of the lens, and an analysis of structures along the line of sight (e.g., <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R22\">Suyu et al. 2014</a>; <a name=\"InR8\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R8\">Greene et al. 2013</a>; <a name=\"InR6\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R6\">Fadely et al. 2010</a>; <a name=\"InR26\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R26\">Treu &amp; Koopmans 2002</a>; <a name=\"InR7\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R7\">Falco et al. 1997</a>; <a name=\"InR9\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R9\">Keeton &amp; Kochanek 1997</a>). Integral field spectroscopy coupled with the adaptive optics that is becoming available on the VLT and at the Keck observatory will be essential in this respect. One of the key ingredient for the method to work at all is, however, the quality of the time-delay measurements, which is the focus of the present work. </p>\n<p>In practice, measuring time delays is achievable if the lensed source is photometrically variable. Gravitationally lensed quasars are ideal targets: the quasars can show variability accessible by moderately sized ground-based optical telescopes on timescales of a few days, while the time delays resulting from galaxy-scale lenses are typically of the order of weeks to months (see, e.g., <a name=\"InR16\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R16\">Oguri &amp; Marshall 2010</a>). The intrinsic light curve of the quasar is seen shifted by the relative time delays in the different lensed images. However, this simple situation is often contaminated: microlensing by stars in the lensing galaxy introduces extrinsic variability in the individual light curves with an amplitude sometimes comparable with that of the intrinsic variability of the quasar. To yield competitive cosmological constraints, reliable time-delay measurements with percent-level precision are needed (<a name=\"InR25\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R25\">Treu 2010</a>). An efficient implementation of these measurements has long been hampered by how difficult it is to obtain photometric data for periods of many years at a desirable cadence, which must be close to 1 epoch per few days (<a name=\"InR4\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R4\">Eigenbrod et al. 2005</a>). </p>\n<p>COSMOGRAIL is a monitoring program targeting more than 30 strongly lensed quasars using meter-class telescopes, with a cadence of 3 days for the most interesting systems. Recent results include light curves and time-delay measurements that are accurate to within a few percent points in HE 0435<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8722;</span>1223 (<a name=\"InR2\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R2\">Courbin et al. 2011</a>), SDSS J1001<span class=\"simple-math\">+</span>5027 (<a name=\"InR17\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R17\">Rathna Kumar et al. 2013</a>) and in RX J1131<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8722;</span>1231 (<a name=\"InR24\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R24\">Tewes et al. 2013b</a>). To measure these time delays, we developed and implemented several algorithms in the form of a COSMOGRAIL curve-shifting toolbox named PyCS, described in <a name=\"InR23\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R23\">Tewes et al. (2013a)</a>. </p>\n<p>In the fall of 2013, a first blind time-delay measurement competition named Time Delay Challenge 1 (TDC1) was proposed to the community by <a name=\"InR3\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R3\">Dobler et al. (2015)</a>. The two main goals of this open challenge were (1) to homogeneously assess the quality of time-delay measurement algorithms on a common set of realistic synthetic light curves, and (2) to obtain some quantitative informations on the impact of observing strategy (cadence, season length, campaign length) on time-delay measurements. We took part in this challenge and submitted time-delay estimates using the team name PyCS. <a name=\"InR10\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R10\">Liao et al. (2015)</a> give a summary of the results from all TDC1 participating teams, as well as some general conclusions. The present paper is complementary to <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R10\">Liao et al. (2015)</a>. It focuses on the PyCS methods that we also apply to real light curves, and hence assesses the quality and reliability of the COSMOGRAIL time-delay measurements. </p>\n<p>To evaluate our existing techniques with the thousands of light curves of TDC1 under conditions similar to the analysis of COSMOGRAIL data, we separated the problem of time-delay measurement of a light curve pair into two successive stages.</p>\n<p>\n        <ul style=\"list-style-type: none;\">\n          <li>\n            Stage I: \n            <p class=\"ligne\"> we first attempt to discover a plausible time delay, withouttrying to measuring it precisely. We also evaluated how confidentwe were that the proposed approximate solution is close to the truetime delay and not a catastrophic failure. Owing to the limitedlength of the monitoring seasons, the randomness of quasarvariability, noise and microlensing, this was not possible forevery light curve pair of TDC1 or a real monitoring campaign. Wenote that in the case of TDC1 we had no prior information on thetime delay to look for, as we had no knowledge of the massdistribution in the lensing galaxy. Only the light curvesthemselves were used. </p>\n          </li>\n          <li>\n            Stage II: \n            <p class=\"ligne\"> for those systems for which Stage I was successful, we then focused on accurately estimating the time delay and associated uncertainty with the PyCS techniques, constraining the algorithms to a delay range around the solution from Stage I. As the PyCS Stage II methods did not rely on a physical model of the light curves, they would not be able to deal adequately with comparing odds among very different solutions. </p>\n          </li>\n        </ul>\n      </p>\n<p>This two-stage structure is of general interest beyond PyCS, as the stages concern discernible aspects of the time-delay measurement problem. Stage I deals with the quantity and the purity of time-delay measurements, while Stage II deals with their actual accuracy. </p>\n<p>The paper is structured as follows. Section <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S2\">2</a> summarizes the data from the TDC1 and the metrics used to evaluate techniques. In Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S3\">3</a>, we present the approaches we took to address Stage I, while Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S6\">4</a> presents the Stage II algorithms. In Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S9\">5</a> we discuss the results, expanding on the analysis of <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R10\">Liao et al. (2015)</a>, and we conclude in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S13\">6</a>. </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S2\"></a>2. Time Delay Challenge 1</h2>\n<p>The mock data used in this work are synthetic quasar light curves made publicly available in the context of the TDC1 proposed by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R3\">Dobler et al. (2015)</a>. These data mimic the photometric variations seen in real gravitationally lensed quasars, with different time sampling, number of seasons, and season length. The curves are generated with simple yet plausible noise properties, and include microlensing variability. The dataset is split into five &#8220;rungs&#8221; or stages that simulate different observational strategies, each rung consisting of 1024 pairs of light curves. The rungs randomly mix microlensing, noise properties, and variability but differ in time sampling, number of seasons, and season length. These differences are listed in Table 1 of <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R10\">Liao et al. (2015, hereafter TDC1 paper)</a>. </p>\n<p>Participants to the TDC1 were asked to provide their best point estimate <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq7.png\"></span> and associated 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> uncertainty <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#948;</i><sub><i>i</i></sub></span> for as many pairs of curves as possible. The submitted entries to the challenge were then compared to the true input delays, and evaluated using simple metrics probing different properties of the estimates. The details of how the simulations were set up, as well as a discussion of these metrics are given in <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R3\">Dobler et al. (2015)</a>. Results obtained by the blind submissions of the different participating teams are summarized in the TDC1 paper, including our submissions denoted &#8220;PyCS&#8221;. For completeness, we briefly summarize the four main metrics:</p>\n<p><ul style=\"list-style-type: none;\">\n<li>1. <p class=\"ligne\"> the fraction <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>f</i></span> of submitted time delay estimates, <a name=\"FD1\"></a><span class=\"img-equation\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq11.png\"><span class=\"label-eq\">(1)</span></span>where <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>N</i><sub>submit</sub></span> is the number of measured time delays and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>N</i></span> is the total number of curves. </p>\n</li>\n<li>2. <p class=\"ligne\"> the mean <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> between the measured time delay <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq7.png\"></span> and the true value <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>t</i><sub><i>i</i></sub></span> weighted using the estimated uncertainties <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#948;</i><sub><i>i</i></sub></span>, <a name=\"FD2\"></a><span class=\"img-equation\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq15.png\"><span class=\"label-eq\">(2)</span></span></p>\n</li>\n<li>3. <p class=\"ligne\"> the mean &#8220;claimed&#8221; precision <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span> of the time-delay measurements, computed from the estimated uncertainties <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#948;</i><sub><i>i</i></sub></span>, <a name=\"FD3\"></a><span class=\"img-equation\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq17.png\"><span class=\"label-eq\">(3)</span></span></p>\n</li>\n<li>4. <p class=\"ligne\"> the mean accuracy <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span> of the time-delay measurements, <a name=\"FD4\"></a><span class=\"img-equation\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq19.png\"><span class=\"label-eq\">(4)</span></span></p>\n</li>\n</ul>To analyse the results in greater detail, we also introduce two modified metrics. First, a &#8220;blind&#8221; precision, <a name=\"FD5\"></a><span class=\"img-equation\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq20.png\"><span class=\"label-eq\">(5)</span></span>where we replace in Eq. (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#FD3\">3</a>) the true value of <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>t</i><sub><i>i</i></sub></span> by its estimation <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq21.png\"></span>. This metric can be computed without knowing of the true time delays; its summation terms are useful, for instance to sort light curve pairs of a real monitoring survey. Second, we introduce a variant of the accuracy <a name=\"FD6\"></a><span class=\"img-equation\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq22.png\"><span class=\"label-eq\">(6)</span></span>where we replace the <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>t</i><sub><i>i</i></sub></span> in the denominator of Eq. (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#FD4\">4</a>) by its absolute value. While <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span> is sensitive to a bias on the amplitude of <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq21.png\"></span> (i.e., over- or underestimation of delays), <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i><sub>abs</sub></span> responds to signed biases. </p>\n<p>Statistical uncertainties on these metrics are computed following <a name=\"FD7\"></a><span class=\"img-equation\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq25.png\"><span class=\"label-eq\">(7)</span></span>where <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i><sub>X</sub></span> is the sample standard deviation of the summation terms of <span class=\"simple-math\">X = <i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span>, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span>, and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span>. </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S3\"></a>3. Stage I: discovering time delays</h2>\n<p>To apply any of the PyCS time-delay measurement algorithms (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R23\">Tewes et al. 2013a</a>) to a pair of light curves, a prior estimate of the time delay is required. Depending on the considered light curves, identifying this delay from the data might be difficult or even impossible. In the following, we describe two approaches to discover rough time-delay estimates (Stage I). Both methods rely solely on the light curves without considering the configuration of the lens system. The first method is based on a visual inspection of the light curves and is the method we used to blindly prepare submissions for the TDC1 (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R10\">Liao et al. 2015</a>). We developed the second method after the TDC1 results were released. We use the data from the challenge to evaluate the relative merits and drawbacks of each method. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S4\"></a>3.1. D3CS: D3 visual curve shifting</h3>\n<p>This method is based on visual inspection of the light curves, in the spirit of citizen science projects (e.g., see the review by <a name=\"InR13\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R13\">Marshall et al. 2015</a>). To ease this process, we developed a dedicated browser-based visualization interface, using the D3.js JavaScript library<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#FN2\">1</a></sup> by <a name=\"InR1\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R1\">Bostock et al. (2011)</a>. We have made this interface public<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#FN3\">2</a></sup>. </p>\n<p>The main motivations behind this time-costly yet simple approach were (1) to obtain rough initial estimates for the time delays and their associated uncertainties, and (2) to estimate how confident one can be that the time-delay estimations are not catastrophic outliers. Clearly, visual curve-shifting allows for more freedom than any automated procedure. It also permits dealing in a more flexible way with unusual behaviour of light curves, such as highly variable signal-to-noise from one season to the next, extreme microlensing, or even when the time delays are comparable in length to the visibility period of the object. </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F1\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F1.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-fig1_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F1.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 1</span></a><p><i>Left</i><i> panel</i>: stacked histogram of the errors made by the visual time-delay estimation, for the secure and <tt>plausible D3CS </tt>samples. All the secure estimations are within the displayed range of errors of <span class=\"simple-math\">&#177; 20</span> days. Only <span class=\"simple-math\">2.6%</span> of the time delays in the plausible sample have an absolute error larger than 20 days. <i>Right panel</i>: absolute time-delay error made by D3CS as a function of the true delay for both samples.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=1&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201526704\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>Our interface allows users to interactively shift the light curves in time, magnitude, and flux, and to zoom in on sections of the data. It permits the visual estimation of the time delay and of an associated uncertainty. Importantly, the interface also asks to pick one of four choices of confidence category for the proposed solution:</p>\n<p>\n          <ul style=\"list-style-type: none;\">\n            <li>\n              1. \n              <p class=\"ligne\">secure: if a catastrophic error can be excluded with a very highconfidence<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#FN4\">3</a></sup>; </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              2. \n              <p class=\"ligne\">plausible: if the solution yields a good fit and no other solutions are seen; </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              3. \n              <p class=\"ligne\">multimodal: if the proposed solution is only one among two or more possible solutions that are equally satisfactory; </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              4. \n              <p class=\"ligne\">uninformative: if the data does not allow the estimate of any time delay. </p>\n            </li>\n          </ul>\n        </p>\n<p>Unlike massive crowdsourcing programs (e.g. <tt>Galaxy Zoo;</tt><a name=\"InR12\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R12\">Lintott et al. 2008</a>), only four scientists participated in the visual inspection of TDC1 and each pair of curves was measured by at least two independent scientists. The behaviour of different users in terms of time spent per time-delay measurement span a broad range. Fast users spend on average 25 s per object, while slower users spend more than 60 s per object. This includes the time taken to measure a delay, to estimate a 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> uncertainty, and to allocate one of the four confidence levels described above. </p>\n<p>To obtain a single Stage I estimation for each light curve pair, we reduce the results obtained by all four scientists in a very conservative way. We systematically downgrade to multimodal the confidence category of pairs with conflicting time-delay estimates. </p>\n<p>We define samples combining the four confidence categories as follows: sec stands for secure only, secpla stands for <tt>secure + plausible</tt>, and secplamul for <tt>secure + plausible + multimodal</tt>. The combination of all estimations is labelled all. </p>\n<p>Figure <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F1\">1</a> shows the distribution of the error on the time-delay estimation versus the true time delay for the secure and <tt>plausible D3CS </tt>subsamples. Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#T1\">1</a> summarizes the results of the D3CS classification and displays the fraction of catastrophic outliers <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#1013;</i></span>, i.e. time-delay estimations more than 20 days away from the truth. Notably, the secure sample contains 1623 time-delay estimates free of any catastrophic outliers. </p>\n<p>\n          <a name=\"T1\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><div class=\"ligne\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/T1.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Table 1</span></a><p>D3CS classification of the TDC1 light curve pairs.</p>\n</div></div>\n        </p>\n<p>Through this simple experiment, we have demonstrated that such an approach is easily manageable for about 5000 light curves. In total the four scientists involved in the visual estimation of the time delays spent 150 h measuring the 5120 delays. We note that 30<span class=\"simple-math\">%</span> of the time delays were measured by three or more users. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S5\"></a>3.2. Attempt to design an automated Stage I procedure</h3>\n<p>Visual inspection of the light curves is a time-consuming process that cannot be repeated many times. Designing an automated method whose efficiency approaches that of D3CS is therefore complementary and would help to minimize the time spent on the visual inspection. We developed such a method after the end of TDC1. The concept of the method is to estimate a time delay by fitting a spline on one of the two light curves, and computing the residual signal of the second light curve relative to the spline after applying time and magnitude shifts. The details of the method are described in Appendix A; the present section evaluates its performance and compares this estimation to the visual time-delay values. </p>\n<p>We characterize the efficiency of the automated Stage I procedure by comparing its fraction of catastrophic outliers <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#1013;</i></span> with that of D3CS. We define catastrophic outliers as time-delay estimations deviating from the truth by more than 20 days, i.e. with <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq32.png\"></span>. The time-delay and confidence estimation evaluated by the automated procedure are reduced to two numbers: the depth of the absolute minimum <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i></span> and the interseason variations of the microlensing <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#958;</i></span>. Figure <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F2\">2</a> shows the evolution of the fraction of catastrophic outliers <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#1013;</i></span> as a function of the cumulative number of time-delay estimations, sorted by increasing <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i></span>. The larger <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i></span> is, the more confident the automated procedure in the time-delay estimation is. We study the impact of the automated procedure parameters <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i></span> and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#958;</i></span> by introducing three subsamples of automated estimations:</p>\n<p>\n          <ul class=\"dash\">\n            <li>\n               \n              <p>the Crude-all subsample contains all the estimations; </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              <p>the Crude-1min subsample contains only the estimations for which the procedure finds a unique local minimum with a depth <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i>&lt;</span>1; </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              <p>the Crude-1.5<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#958;</i></span> subsample contains only the estimations with a magnitude shift deviation <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#958;</i>&lt; 1.5</span> at the location of the absolute minimum <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i></span>. </p>\n            </li>\n          </ul>\n        </p>\n<p>Figure <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F2\">2</a> shows the fraction of outliers <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#1013;</i></span> in the three subsamples and compares them to the value obtained visually with D3CS, which are shown as four diamond-shaped symbols corresponding to the combination of the four confidence categories of D3CS described in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S4\">3.1</a>. We note that the uninformative D3CS estimations are systematically considered as catastrophic outliers here. </p>\n<p>The selection criteria applied to the Crude-1min and Crude-1.5<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#958;</i></span> subsamples are not able to decrease the fraction of outliers. This highlights how difficult it is to find efficient selection criteria for the automated procedure parameters, although no exhaustive exploration of the parameters space has been conducted. As expected, all the D3CS subsamples contain fewer outliers than the corresponding automated procedure subsamples. However, the efficiency of the latter are of the same order as the other automated methods presented in the TDC1 paper, which have <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#1013;</i> = 2&#8722;3</span>% when half of the TDC1 data, i.e. 2500 light curve pairs, are measured. </p>\n<p>In conclusion, although the automated procedure presented here is less complete and reliable than D3CS, it yields candidates that can be evaluated by eye in a second phase. Such a combined approach would benefit both from the speed of the automated method and from the flexibility of the human eye estimate when dealing with a broad variety of properties in the light curves. We note, however, that in the rest of the paper, we only use the results obtained via D3CS as our Stage I estimates. </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F2\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F2.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-fig2_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F2.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 2</span></a><p>Cumulative evolution of the fraction of catastrophic outliers <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#1013;</i></span> (in percentage points) as a function of the number of time-delay estimations. To produce the plot, the curves are first sorted according to the depth of their absolute minimum <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i></span>, indicated in the colour bar. Each black line (solid, dashed and dotted) represents a different subsample (see text for details). The coloured diamonds show the value of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#1013;</i></span> for the D3CS combined samples; the corresponding number of estimations are indicated in parenthesis.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=2&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201526704\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S6\"></a>4. Stage II: measuring time delays</h2>\n<p>Using the Stage I results as initial estimates, we proceed in this section by running our PyCS time-delay measurement techniques on the simulated TDC1 light curves. In <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R23\">Tewes et al. (2013a)</a>, three different algorithms were proposed: the simultaneous fit of the light curves using free-knot splines, the regression difference technique, and an approach based on a dispersion measurement of which the free-knot splines and the regression difference technique yielded the most accurate and precise results when applied to simulated COSMOGRAIL data (in <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R2\">Courbin et al. 2011</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R24\">Tewes et al. 2013b</a>; <a name=\"InR5\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R5\">Eulaers et al. 2013</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R17\">Rathna Kumar et al. 2013</a>). To analyse the TDC1 simulations, we have therefore focused on adapting only these two most promising methods for an automated use. </p>\n<p>We note again that our Stage II methods cannot be asked to judge the plausibility of a proposed delay. This step belongs to the Stage I method, i.e. to the visual inspection with D3CS to prevent or at least reduce catastrophic outliers. In practice, despite a correct Stage I estimate, any automated Stage II method may fail to converge, or it may yield a time-delay measurement that is incompatible with the initial approximation. To prevent these failures from contaminating our measurements we systematically discard any Stage II result that does not lie within 1.5 D3CS uncertainty estimate of the D3CS point estimate. This threshold acknowledges that the uncertainty estimates obtained from D3CS are typically overestimated by a factor of 2 to 3, which has been confirmed by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R10\">Liao et al. (2015)</a>. We note that this rejection affects less than 1% of the light curve pairs and has no significant influence on the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>f</i></span> metric. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S7\"></a>4.1. Free-knot spline technique</h3>\n<p>In the free-knot spline technique (spl), each light curve in a pair is modelled as the sum of a spline representing the intrinsic variability of the quasar, common to both images of the pair, and an independent spline representing the extrinsic variability due to microlensing. The intrinsic spline has a higher density of knots and is therefore more flexible accomodating the quasar variability, which is assumed to be faster than the microlensing variability. During the iterative fitting process, the light curves are shifted in time so as to optimize the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> between the data and the model light curve. To analyse a TDC1 light curve pair, we repeat this fit 20 times, starting form different initial conditions covering the Stage I uncertainty. This tests the robustness of the optimization procedure. The best model fit is then used to generate 40 simulated noisy light curves with a range of true time delays around the best-fit solution and using the temporal sampling of the original light curves. By blindly rerunning the spline fit on these simulated data, and comparing the resulting delays with the true input time delays, the delay measurement uncertainty is estimated. </p>\n<p>We simplified and automated the spl algorithm for TDC1 with respect to the description of the free-knot spline method given in (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R23\">Tewes et al. 2013a</a>) and its applications to real COSMOGRAIL data. The main adaptations are the following:</p>\n<p>\n          <ul style=\"list-style-type: none;\">\n            <li>\n              1. \n              <p class=\"ligne\"> The temporal density of spline knots controlling the flexibilityof the intrinsic spline was computed from the signal-to-noiseratios measured on the two light curves, using an empiricalcalibration. The signal-to-noise ratios were obtained from astructure function, by comparing the typical amplitude of thelight curve variability observed on a timescale of 50 to 75 dayswith the scatter between temporally adjacent observing epochs.For the microlensing spline, the knot density was fixed to be thesame for all TDC1 pairs. </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              2. \n              <p class=\"ligne\"> When generating our mock light curves, we did not inject any fast microlensing signal to mimic correlated noise. Only plain white noise was added to the generative model. </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              3. \n              <p class=\"ligne\"> We did not analyse the time-delay measurement errors on the simulated curves as a function of true time delay. Instead, only the RMS error of these time-delay measurements was used as our total uncertainty estimate. </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              4. \n              <p class=\"ligne\"> Finally, we did not manually fine-tune any parameters or correct for problematic model fits. </p>\n            </li>\n          </ul>\n        </p>\n<p>As a result, the entire spl analysis took about 5 CPU-minutes per TDC1 pair. </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F3\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F3.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-fig3_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F3.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 3</span></a><p> Quantitative analysis of the discoverability of time delays through the extensive visual search with D3CS (Stage I) in the case of four-month observing seasons and a cadence of 3 days. The coloured tiles show the fraction of discovered delays as a function of the photometric precision of the fainter quasar image and the true time delay of the system. <i>Left panel </i>shows results from the very conservative sec sample, and <i>central panel </i>shows the less pure secpla sample that includes delay candidates considered as plausible (see text). <i>Right panel</i>, also for secpla, doubles the number of observing seasons. In each panel, only tiles covering more than three light curve pairs are shown.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=3&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201526704\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S8\"></a>4.2. Regression difference with splines</h3>\n<p>Our second Stage II method, sdi (for spline difference), is based on the regression difference technique of <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R23\">Tewes et al. (2013a)</a>. To speed up the analysis, we replace the Gaussian process regressions by spline fits. In summary, the method independently fits a different spline to each of the two light curves, and then minimizes the variability of the difference between these two splines by shifting them in time with respect to each other. The advantage of this approach is that it does not require an explicit microlensing model. To estimate the uncertainty, the sdi method is run on the same simulated light curves provided by the spl technique. The sdi method has an even lower computational cost than spl. </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F4\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F4.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-fig4_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F4.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 4</span></a><p>Summary of metrics obtained with the Stage II algorithms spl and sdi, without any a posteriori clipping of the outliers. <i>Bottom row </i>presents enlargements taken from the panels on the <i>upper row</i>. The shaded regions represent the somewhat arbitrary target areas that were defined in the TDC1 paper.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=4&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201526704\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F5\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F5.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-fig5_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F5.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 5</span></a><p>Quantitative analysis of the precision achieved for the Stage II time-delay measurement as a function of the photometric precision of the fainter quasar image and as a function of the true time delay. All panels show results from the bias-free spl technique for the <tt>secure + plausible </tt>selection of rungs 2 and 3 after rejection of the catastrophic outliers (see text). <i>Top left panel </i>shows the metric <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span> as computed using the uncertainty estimates <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#948;</i><sub><i>i</i></sub></span> without using <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq21.png\"></span>. <i>Top right panel </i>shows the rms of the relative point estimation residuals without considering <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#948;</i><sub><i>i</i></sub></span>. <i>Bottom left panel </i>shows the average <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span> obtained in each tile after selecting only the best half of systems according to the blind precision in <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq38.png\"></span>. <i>Bottom right panel </i>shows a map of the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> metric. In all panels, only tiles describing more than three light curve pairs are shown.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=5&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201526704\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S9\"></a>5. Results on the TDC1 data</h2>\n<p>In this section, we separately analyse results from the Stage I and Stage II measurement processes as obtained on the simulated light curves of TDC1. General results from <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R10\">Liao et al. (2015)</a> regarding submissions prepared with our methods include the following:</p>\n<p>\n        <ul style=\"list-style-type: none;\">\n          <li>\n            1. \n            <p class=\"ligne\"> The Stage II methods spl and sdi show no significantdeviations of the accuracy <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span> from zero, and can thus be considered as inherently unbiased, given the statistical limits due to the finite challenge size. </p>\n          </li>\n          <li>\n            2. \n            <p class=\"ligne\"> The claimed precisions <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span> of spl and sdi are very good, with <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> values of the order of <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq39.png\"></span> and <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq40.png\"></span>. </p>\n          </li>\n          <li>\n            3. \n            <p class=\"ligne\"> Based on results from the <span class=\"simple-math\">spl</span> method simple power-law models for the dependence of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span>, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span>, and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>f</i></span> on monitoring cadence, season length, and campaign length were adjusted. These relations attempt to capture general trends regarding the behaviour of all methods used in the context of TDC1, including our spl technique. </p>\n          </li>\n        </ul>\n      </p>\n<p>In the present paper, we focus on aspects that are complementary to the discussion of <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R10\">Liao et al. (2015)</a>. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S10\"></a>5.1. Efficiency of time-delay discovery (Stage I)</h3>\n<p>We start by analysing the fraction of light curve pairs for which a time delay can be discovered with visual inspection, as a function of time delay and image brightness. This analysis relates only to the first stage of the time-delay measurement process. </p>\n<p>Aside from the time delay and the quasar image brightness, the question of discoverability depends on observational conditions (e.g. monitoring cadence and duration) and on astrophysical characteristics (e.g. amount of quasar variability and microlensing perturbations). In the following, we select a given observing strategy and average over the astrophysical parameters of the TDC1 simulations, which follow clearly motivated distributions (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R3\">Dobler et al. 2015</a>). A large sample of light curve pairs with almost identical observing conditions can be obtained by merging rungs 2 and 3. These rungs share the fiducial three-day monitoring cadence for five seasons of four months each. The differing cadence dispersion of <span class=\"simple-math\">0.0</span> days for rung 2 and <span class=\"simple-math\">1.0</span> days for rung 3 (Table 1 of <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R10\">Liao et al. 2015</a>) do not have a significant impact on the discoverability of time delays. </p>\n<p>In practice, time delays can be measured accurately in pairs of light curves if the quality of both light curves is sufficient. In the following we consider as a relevant observable the photometric precision achieved in the fainter image of a pair. This is computed for each pair of light curves, as the median of the photometric error bars across the epochs of the TDC1 simulations. This is made legitimate by their overall effectiveness in representing the amplitude of the simulated noise, except for very few &#8220;evil&#8221; epochs of some systems (see Sect. 2.5 of <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R10\">Liao et al. 2015</a>). However, when analysing real light curves, using the photometric scatter between the points might be a better choice than using potentially mis-estimated photometric error bars. </p>\n<p>Figure <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F3\">3</a> presents the distribution of the fraction of light curve pairs for which time delays could be identified via a meticulous D3CS visual inspection for two different monitoring strategies. In the left panel, only time delays categorized as secure through the visual inspection are considered as discovered. This is very conservative because in a real survey, simple lens models will help to identify the correct time delay for almost all of the plausible systems as well. For this reason we also consider the combined secpla sample, shown in the central panel. </p>\n<p>Some of the cases categorized as multimodal could certainly also be resolved using simple lens model considerations, but in practice the vast majority of these light curve pairs contain too few clear common features to estimate a reliable time delay, even if an approximate value would be known from the modelling. We therefore consider the discoverability of the secpla selection shown in the central panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F3\">3</a> as roughly representative of the fraction of potentially helpful delays that can be reliably measured from a real monitoring campaign or survey. It can be seen as an approximate lower limit for the fraction of time delays that can be observationally constrained in the absence of prior from a lens model, provided the properties of the microlensing signal are similar to those of the simulated light curves used here. Finally, the right panel shows the evolution of this discoverability if the same monitoring strategy is carried out for five more seasons, i.e., for a total of ten years. </p>\n<p>We observe that after five years of monitoring, light curve pairs with a photometric precision in the fainter image better than <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i> = 0.1</span> mag and a true time delay shorter than <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>t</i> = 80</span> days (2/3 of the season length) are very likely to yield a time-delay measurement. Pursuing the monitoring for five more years significantly increases the average chances that longer delays up to <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span><span class=\"simple-math\">90%</span> of the season length become measurable. </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F6\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F6.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-fig6_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F6.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 6</span></a><p>Evolution of the TDC1 metrics per rung as a function of the fraction of estimations <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>f</i></span> for the spl-sec and sdi-sec samples. The plots are sorted by increasing values of the blind precision <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq38.png\"></span> (see text). Shaded regions represent the error on the metrics. Solid grey lines show the target values for the metrics as defined in the TDC1 paper. Dashed grey lines show the best possible value for each metric. The <i>bottom row </i>presents the non-cumulative evolution of the median of the true time delays <span class=\"simple-math\">| &#916;<i>t</i><sub><i>i</i></sub> |</span> in bins of ten estimations.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=6&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201526704\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S11\"></a>5.2. Precision of time-delay measurement (Stage II)</h3>\n<p>We now turn to the analysis of the time-delay measurements (Stage II) for all systems where the time delay is successfully discovered (Stage I). </p>\n<p>Figure <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F4\">4</a> summarizes the results of the spl and sdi methods in terms of the metrics <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span> (accuracy), <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span> (claimed precision), and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span>, as defined in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S2\">2</a>. The figure follows the same conventions as Table 4 of <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R10\">Liao et al. (2015)</a>, but also includes measurements obtained on the secpla samples of each rung. As expected, the results for these secpla samples are more scattered than for the sec samples. The reason for these significant offsets in <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span> and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> with respect to the sec results is the stronger impact of outliers on the non-robust metrics. </p>\n<p>To characterize the achievable precision of the Stage II measurements without being influenced by catastrophic outliers but still benefiting from a large number of light curve pairs, we now focus on the secpla sample from which we remove systems with estimated delays that are off by more than 20 days. This rejects about 1% of the secpla sample. We also note that catastrophic outliers are errors of the Stage I estimate, not Stage II. </p>\n<p>Figure <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F5\">5</a> presents metrics related to the Stage II time-delay measurement precision as a function of the photometric quality of the fainter quasar light curve and the time delay. In each tile the top left panel shows the average claimed precision <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span> for the spl technique, for a five-year monitoring with four-month seasons and a cadence of three days. We find that the cadence dispersion plays no significant role in this analysis, and we therefore merge rungs 2 and 3 to obtain this larger sample. </p>\n<p>In contrast, each tile of the top right panel shows the root mean square (rms) of the relative error of the time-delay estimates <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq49.png\"></span>. The observed structure is inevitably noisier because this rms is computed from the actual point estimates, while the precision <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span> is based on the corresponding claimed uncertainty estimates. We observe both a qualitative and a quantitative similarity between these plots, suggesting that the time-delay uncertainty estimates, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#948;</i><sub><i>i</i></sub></span> (Eq. (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#FD2\">2</a>)), from the PyCS techniques correctly capture trends related to the photometric quality and to the amount of temporal overlap in the light curves. </p>\n<p>In the lower right panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F5\">5</a>, the map of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> metrics for the spl technique shows no strong evolution across the well-sampled regions of the parameter space. It does however indicate that the uncertainty estimates <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#948;</i><sub><i>i</i></sub></span> from spl are too conservative by a small factor of <span class=\"simple-math\">(<i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup>)<sup>&#8722; 1/2</sup> &#8771; 0.5<sup>&#8722; 1/2</sup> &#8771; 1.4</span>. This is particularly visible for the high quality light curves with small time delays, i.e. with large temporal overlaps. Finally, the bottom left panel shows the average <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span> metric computed using only the best half of light curve pairs in each tile, where the quality of a system is judged via the blind relative precision <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq51.png\"></span> (see Eq. (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#FD5\">5</a>)). This operation, aimed at increasing the precision, divides by two the usable fraction of systems as given in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F3\">3</a>. We consider such a selection in more detail in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#S12\">5.3</a>. </p>\n<p>We observe in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F5\">5</a> that the best average relative precision in time-delay measurements seems to be achieved for time delays in the range 40<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8722;</span>80 days for this particular monitoring strategy. This corresponds to about half of the season length, and results from the trade-off between absolute delay length and amount of overlap in the light curves. </p>\n<p>Given the observed general aptitude of our time-delay uncertainty estimates, and thus <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span>, to describe the actual point estimate errors committed by the spl technique, and the excellent competitiveness of spl compared to other time delay measurement techniques (see, e.g., Fig. 13 of <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R10\">Liao et al. 2015</a>), we see the left panels of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F5\">5</a> as roughly representative of the precision that can be achieved by a state-of-the-art time-delay measurement method. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S12\"></a>5.3. Effect on the overall metrics of selecting the best systems</h3>\n<p>In Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F6\">6</a> we show the evolution of the overall average metrics as a function of the fraction of estimations <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>f</i></span> for the spl-sec and sdi-sec samples. The five columns represent the five rungs and the estimations are sorted according to their blind precision <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq38.png\"></span>, i.e the precision estimate from the TDC1 data prior to the unblinding of the true values. The non-cumulative median value of the true delays (bottom row) is computed on consecutive bins of ten estimations. The shaded regions represent the statistical uncertainties of the metrics <span class=\"simple-math\">X<sub>err</sub></span> defined in Eq. (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#FD7\">7</a>). These uncertainties are too small in the top row to be distinguished from the curves. </p>\n<p>In the top row, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span> increases monotonically with <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>f</i></span>. This is expected since the estimations are sorted according to <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq38.png\"></span> and since the <tt>D3CS sec </tt>sample is free of any outliers. The metrics <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span>, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span>, and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i><sub>abs</sub></span>, respectively in the second, third and fourth rows stabilize around a mean value once a significant fraction of estimations is considered. The variations around the mean such as the jump in <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> at <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>f</i> ~ 0.05</span> in rung 2 are due to non-catastrophic outliers, i.e. time delays that deviate significantly from the truth but by less than 20 days. These outliers are the result of a non-optimal convergence of the Stage II methods for the curves with the lowest signal-to-noise. </p>\n<p>The high-<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>f</i></span> end of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span> and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i><sub>abs</sub></span> are subject to strong variations in all rungs. These variations occur for small absolute values of the true time delay <span class=\"simple-math\">| &#916;<i>t</i> |</span>. Similarly, the high-<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>f</i></span> end of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span> increases strongly. A small error on the time-delay estimation particularly affects <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>P</i></span>, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span>, and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i><sub>abs</sub></span> if the true time delay is small. </p>\n<p>This correlation between the loss in precision and accuracy means that for the corresponding light curves, our algorithms estimate the time delays less accurately, but do provide larger error bars. We observe that the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> remains constant as <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>f</i></span> increases. In conclusion, sorting the measurements in <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq38.png\"></span> and rejecting a small fraction of the least precise estimations allows an optimal accuracy to be maintained without affecting the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span>. </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F7\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F7.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-fig7_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/F7.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 7</span></a><p>Evolution of the TDC1 metrics with the fraction of estimations sorted by increasing blind precision <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq38.png\"></span>, for the spl-sec and spl-secpla samples merging all rungs. The spl-secpla-cut sample has been cleaned a posteriori from the outliers in the spl-secpla sample. In doing so, we removed 29 curves from the spl-secpla sample. The shaded regions, the solid and dashed grey lines are the same as in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F6\">6</a>.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=7&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201526704\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>Figure <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F7\">7</a> shows the evolution of the TDC1 metrics with the fraction of estimations, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>f</i></span>, sorted by increasing order of <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15-eq38.png\"></span>, for the spl-sec and spl-secpla sample. The few catastrophic outliers result in striking discontinuities in the curves. Quantifying the accuracy and precision of Stage II methods is different from avoiding such catastrophic outliers, and to address the former question, we also display in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#F7\">7</a> a new subsample, spl-secpla-cut, where the 29 time-delay estimates with an absolute time-delay error larger than 20 days are a posteriori removed. Similarly, the impact of outliers can be reduced by considering the median of the individual metrics instead of their mean. This is not surprising, but nevertheless it reflects the need either to use metrics that can cope with outliers or, as in our Stage I approach, to make sure that no outliers contaminate the time-delay samples used for subsequent cosmological application. </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S13\"></a>6. Summary and conclusions</h2>\n<p>In this work, we used the simulated light curves of the TDC1 proposed by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R3\">Dobler et al. (2015)</a> to test the performance of the PyCS numerical techniques currently in use in COSMOGRAIL to measure time delays. These methods are fully data-driven, in the sense that they do not attempt to include any physical model for microlensing or for the intrinsic variations of quasars. This choice was deliberate and considers an empirical representation of the data that minimizes the risk of bias due to choosing the wrong physical model. The price to pay is that error bars on the measurements must be estimated from mocks and that we cannot use prior knowledge from external observations of quasars in a direct formal way. Using the same simulated light curves, we also assessed the quantity and quality of the time-delay measurements from future monitoring campaigns or surveys. We have made public our six main TDC1 submissions, obtained using the D3CS, spl, and sdi methods for the high purity secure and the less conservative plausible samples. These data are available on the COSMOGRAIL website<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#FN5\">4</a></sup>. Our results can be summarized as follows: </p>\n<p><ul style=\"list-style-type: none;\">\n          <li>\n            1. \n            <p class=\"ligne\"> The visual estimation of time delays (Stage I) is extremely efficient in spotting\n              catastrophic outliers and in providing useful time-delay estimates to be refined with\n              subsequent numerical techniques (Stage II). </p>\n          </li>\n          <li>\n            2. \n            <p class=\"ligne\"> We attempted to build a simple automated time-delay estimation procedure that we can\n              apply to the TDC1 data. While useful, this automated procedure does not achieve as\n              good purity in the time-delay sample as the visual estimation. We note that we did not\n              use this automated procedure for any of our submissions to the TDC1. </p>\n          </li>\n          <li>\n            3. \n            <p class=\"ligne\"> We provide a general analysis of the achievability of time-delay measurements as a\n              function of the photometric precision of the light curves. In particular we show that\n              almost all time delays shorter than two-thirds of the season length can be measured in\n              five years of monitoring with four-month seasons and realistic photometric quality.\n            </p>\n          </li>\n          <li>\n            4. \n            <p class=\"ligne\"> Our Stage II methods spl and sdi can\n              be considered unbiased given the statistical limits due to the finite challenge size.\n              The <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> values are close to unity. These\n              good results emphasize the reliability of COSMOGRAIL time-delay measurements in\n              general. </p>\n          </li>\n          <li>\n            5. \n            <p class=\"ligne\"> We quantify the average precision on the time-delay measurements as a function of\n              photometric quality of the light curves. We find that the best average precision seems\n              to be obtained for pairs whose time delay is approximately half of the monitoring\n              season length. </p>\n          </li>\n          <li>\n            6. \n            <p class=\"ligne\"> We show that we can reliably evaluate the individual precision of our time-delay\n              estimates. This may enable us, for any sample of light curves, to identify which are\n              the most valuable objects to be followed up for cosmological purposes. We note,\n              however, that any selection on the time delays in a quasar sample may also result in\n              biases on the cosmological inference. </p>\n          </li>\n        </ul>The above is true for the specific light curves of TDC1. These curves have been generated with simple models for quasar variations and microlensing and they do not include all potential nuisances of astrophysical, atmospheric, or instrumental origin. In addition, the PyCS techniques currently used in COSMOGRAIL do not attempt to account for these effects. </p>\n    <hr width=\"30%\" align=\"left\">\n<div><div>\n<a name=\"FN2\"></a><sup>1</sup> <p class=\"ligne\"> Data-Driven Documents, <a href=\"http://www.d3js.org/\" target=\"_blank\">http://www.d3js.org/</a></p>\n</div></div>\n<div><div>\n<a name=\"FN3\"></a><sup>2</sup> <p class=\"ligne\"><a href=\"http://www.astro.uni-bonn.de/~mtewes/d3cs/tdc1/\" target=\"_blank\">http://www.astro.uni-bonn.de/~mtewes/d3cs/tdc1/</a> (See &#8220;Read me first&#8221; for help).</p>\n</div></div>\n<div><div>\n<a name=\"FN4\"></a><sup>3</sup> <p class=\"ligne\"> This same category was named doubtless in <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/01/aa26704-15/aa26704-15.html#R10\">Liao et al. (2015)</a>.</p>\n</div></div>\n<div><div>\n<a name=\"FN5\"></a><sup>4</sup> <p class=\"ligne\">\n          <a href=\"http://www.cosmograil.org/tdc1\" target=\"_blank\">http://www.cosmograil.org/tdc1</a>\n        </p>\n</div></div>\n    <h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"ack\"></a>Acknowledgments</h2>\n<p>The authors would like to thank the organizers of the Time Delay Challenge, as well as Peter Schneider for useful discussions and the anonymous referee for his/her useful comments.
